Marshall's review in Science (2013) represents the mainstream paleontological response: the Cambrian explosion is a genuine anomaly but not one that defeats evolutionary theory, because the relevant mechanisms (regulatory evolution) were not available to Darwin and are now better understood. Meyer's response to Marshall (available on the Discovery Institute website) extends the argument. The honest assessment: the Cambrian explosion is a real and acknowledged puzzle in evolutionary biology, not a manufactured controversy; whether it specifically requires ID or merely incomplete evolutionary theory is genuinely debated by scientists, not just philosophers.

Connections to the Course

Week 6 (Catholicism and Science). The second volume in Meyer's ID trilogy, building on Signature in the Cell and setting up Return of the God Hypothesis.

Notable Quote
"The Cambrian explosion — Darwin's doubt — has not been resolved by subsequent fossil discoveries. It has, if anything, become more acute."— Stephen C. Meyer, Darwin's Doubt (2013)
Actionable Takeaways
  • Read Marshall's Science review alongside Meyer's book — this is what rigorous scientific engagement with ID looks like from the mainstream side.
  • The Cambrian explosion is a real phenomenon worth knowing about regardless of where you land on ID — it's a significant feature of the history of life.
  • The distinction between "current evolutionary theory cannot explain X" and "evolution cannot in principle explain X" is crucial — Meyer argues the latter, critics say only the former.
Central Claim

Three major scientific discoveries — cosmological fine-tuning, the digital information in DNA, and the emergence of consciousness — together form a cumulative case that the universe had a beginning, was finely tuned for life by a prior intelligence, and that mind precedes matter rather than arising from it. These three lines of evidence converge on theism as the best explanation.

Supporting Arguments

Cosmological Fine-Tuning: The fundamental constants of physics (gravitational constant, electromagnetic force, the cosmological constant, the ratio of proton to electron mass, and dozens of others) are set to extraordinary precision for life to be possible. Change any by small amounts and the universe produces no stars, no chemistry, no life. The probability of this by chance is vanishingly small. The main alternative — the multiverse — is unobservable, unfalsifiable, and speculative.

The Big Bang and the Beginning: Modern cosmology confirms that the universe had a beginning (the Big Bang, further evidenced by cosmic expansion, the CMB radiation, and the second law of thermodynamics). Whatever begins to exist has a cause. The universe began to exist. Therefore it has a cause — one that transcends space, time, matter, and energy. This is the cosmological argument updated for 21st-century physics.

Biological Information: Extends the Signature in the Cell argument — DNA's information points to prior intelligence at the origin of life.

Consciousness: The hard problem of consciousness (why there is something it is like to be a physical system) resists materialist explanation. Human rational consciousness — including its ability to access abstract mathematical truths — suggests a mind-first rather than matter-first metaphysics.

Key Assumptions

The multiverse hypothesis does not dissolve the fine-tuning argument. Inference to the best explanation is legitimate in cosmology. The three arguments are genuinely independent (not three versions of the same argument) and therefore compound each other's evidential weight.

Counterarguments Addressed

The multiverse gets the most sustained treatment — Meyer argues it is scientifically untestable, philosophically motivated, and actually compounds rather than eliminates the design inference (a finely tuned multiverse-generating mechanism still requires explanation). He engages Richard Dawkins's "who designed the designer?" objection, Stephen Hawking's no-boundary proposal, and Laurence Krauss's "universe from nothing" argument.

The Counterargument Case — From the Other Side

Victor J. Stenger, The Fallacy of Fine-Tuning (2011): Stenger, a particle physicist, makes the most technically detailed case against Meyer's fine-tuning argument. His core claim: the fine-tuning argument depends on varying one constant at a time while holding all others fixed, but the constants of physics are not independent variables — they are related through deeper physical principles we don't yet fully understand. When Stenger varies multiple constants simultaneously in computer simulations, he finds that a significant range of possible universes (up to a third, in some analyses) would be life-permitting, because different combinations of constants can produce stable chemistry. If life-permitting universes are not rare but common across the parameter space, the fine-tuning argument collapses. Meyer responds that Stenger's simulations use a simplistic definition of "life-permitting" and that the deeper the analysis goes, the more finely tuned the constants appear.

Sean Carroll, The Big Picture: On the Origins of Life, Meaning, and the Universe Itself (2016): Carroll, a Caltech physicist, argues that Meyer's three-part case conflates scientific evidence with philosophical interpretation at every step. On fine-tuning: the fact that our universe permits life does not itself require a designer — observers necessarily find themselves in life-permitting universes (the anthropic principle), so the observation carries no evidential weight independent of a prior probability assignment about universes. Carroll's deeper argument: the theistic hypothesis does not actually make predictions that differ from naturalism in any testable way. If God could create any kind of universe, theism is compatible with every possible observation — which means no observation constitutes evidence for theism over naturalism. On consciousness: Carroll defends "poetic naturalism" — the emergence of consciousness from matter is surprising but not logically impossible, and the hard problem of consciousness is a conceptual puzzle rather than an empirical gap requiring a supernatural explanation.

Richard Dawkins, The God Delusion (2006) — "Who Designed the Designer?": Dawkins's central philosophical objection to all design arguments: if the universe's complexity requires a designer, then the designer must be at least as complex as the universe, which means the designer requires a designer, producing an infinite regress. God, on this view, doesn't explain complexity — he relocates it. Meyer's response (which is a standard theistic response) is that God is not a complex physical entity requiring explanation but a necessary being who is simple in the philosophical sense — a mind, not a machine. This is the standard Thomistic response: God is actus purus, pure act, containing no unrealized potency and therefore no complexity in the relevant sense. Whether Dawkins's objection is fatal depends entirely on what kind of God is being posited — it works against some concepts of God and not others.

Lawrence Krauss, A Universe from Nothing (2012): Krauss argues that quantum physics has undermined the cosmological argument's premise that "something cannot come from nothing" — virtual particles appear and disappear from the quantum vacuum constantly, and the entire observable universe could have arisen from a quantum fluctuation in a pre-existing quantum vacuum. Meyer and philosopher David Albert both respond that Krauss's "nothing" is not actual nothing — a quantum vacuum is a rich physical entity with properties, laws, and energy. The debate between Meyer and Krauss is partly terminological (what counts as "nothing?") and partly substantive (can physical law itself have a cause?). Albert's review in the New York Times called Krauss's argument a "sleight of hand" — the objection is substantive regardless of one's theology.

Critical Assessment

The most philosophically sophisticated of Meyer's three books, and the most directly relevant to Catholic natural theology. The fine-tuning argument is taken seriously by physicists who are not ID proponents — Paul Davies, Martin Rees, and Roger Penrose have all written about fine-tuning as a genuine puzzle without endorsing ID specifically. Meyer's cumulative case approach is methodologically strong — the three arguments are genuinely independent. The weakest section is consciousness, where the philosophical argument is real but the connection to intelligent design (as opposed to theism generally) is less clear. The strongest version of the critical response: the God hypothesis doesn't actually explain fine-tuning — it relocates the explanation rather than providing one.

Connections to the Course

Week 6 (Catholicism and Science, Lemaître lecture, Five Ways), Week 3 (Teilhard and the Omega Point, theodicy). The most direct scientific engagement with the cosmological and teleological arguments for God's existence in the course reading list.

Notable Quote
"The evidence from physics, cosmology, and biology converges on the same startling conclusion: the universe gives every indication of having been designed by a prior intelligence."— Stephen C. Meyer, Return of the God Hypothesis (2021)
Actionable Takeaways
  • The three-part framework (cosmology, biology, consciousness) gives you independent lines of evidence to deploy separately in different conversations.
  • The multiverse response to fine-tuning is not as strong as it sounds — learn why before encountering it in debate.
  • Read alongside a sympathetic mainstream physicist treatment of fine-tuning (Paul Davies's The Cosmic Jackpot) and a skeptical response (Victor Stenger's The Fallacy of Fine-Tuning) for a full picture.
Central Claim

Theistic evolution — the position that God used unguided Darwinian evolution to create life, including human beings — is scientifically inadequate, philosophically incoherent, and theologically problematic for orthodox Christianity. This is a multi-author critique of the BioLogos position (associated with Francis Collins and the evolutionary creationism movement).

Supporting Arguments

Scientific Critique (Meyer, Behe, Axe, Gauger): Standard Neo-Darwinian mechanisms are insufficient to produce the new biological information required for macroevolution — the same arguments made in Meyer's solo books, applied specifically to theistic evolution's acceptance of unguided evolution.

Philosophical Critique (Moreland, Meyer): Theistic evolutionists typically claim God directed the evolutionary process while simultaneously affirming that the process was unguided — this is incoherent. If God directed it, it wasn't unguided; if it was unguided, God didn't direct it. The attempt to have both simultaneously fails philosophically.

Theological Critique (Grudem, Collins, Madueme, Williams): Theistic evolution, as typically formulated, requires revising or abandoning: (a) the historical Adam and Eve as the first humans and progenitors of the species; (b) the Fall as a historical event with real moral and physical consequences; (c) the special creation of human beings in the image of God; (d) death as a consequence of sin (Paul's argument in Romans 5 requires a pre-sin deathless world). These revisions have cascading consequences for the doctrines of original sin, atonement, and redemption.

Key Assumptions

The contributors assume that the relevant theistic evolutionists (BioLogos) mean genuinely unguided evolution, not front-loaded design. The theological section assumes a high view of biblical inerrancy. Many Catholic contributors to the broader TE debate would not share these assumptions.

Counterarguments Addressed

Engages directly with Francis Collins (The Language of God), Denis Alexander (Creation or Evolution: Do We Have to Choose?), and Karl Giberson. The book was specifically motivated by perceived inadequacies in existing TE responses to ID critiques.

The Counterargument Case — From the Other Side

Francis Collins, The Language of God: A Scientist Presents Evidence for Belief (2006): Collins, director of the Human Genome Project and later of the NIH, makes the most prominent case for theistic evolution from an evangelical Christian perspective. His central argument: the genome is "the language of God" — its complexity and elegance reveal a Creator, but the mechanism of creation was evolutionary. Collins argues that the moral law (the human sense of altruism and right and wrong that exceeds what evolution would predict) and the fine-tuning of the universe are the genuine evidences for God — not biological complexity, which evolution explains perfectly well. On the origin question that Meyer raises, Collins argues the Meyer/Dembski critique of natural selection misunderstands how evolution actually works: evolution does not require each step to be independently functional at its intermediate stages, because neutral mutations can accumulate without selection until a functional threshold is reached. Collins founded BioLogos in 2007 specifically to provide a home for Christians who accept evolutionary science without abandoning orthodox faith.

Denis Alexander, Creation or Evolution: Do We Have to Choose? (2008): Alexander, a molecular biologist and former director of the Faraday Institute at Cambridge, argues for what he calls "Model A" theistic evolution: God created by and through the evolutionary process, using it as his instrument. Alexander directly rebuts the theological critique in Theistic Evolution — that TE requires abandoning a historical Adam — by arguing that "Adam and Eve" can be read as the first two representatives of Homo sapiens on whom God conferred spiritual status, without requiring them to be the sole progenitors of all humanity. This "federal headship" reading preserves the theological function of the Fall (the introduction of sin into human history through a real historical act of disobedience) without requiring a genetic bottleneck of two individuals that population genetics rules out. Alexander's primary audience is British evangelical scientists, and his theological engagement is more sophisticated than most American TE treatments.

Darrel Falk, Coming to Peace with Science (2004) and the BioLogos Response: Falk, a geneticist and former BioLogos president, argues that the theological objections in Theistic Evolution — especially the charge that TE makes God's action undetectable and therefore meaningless — misunderstand how God acts through secondary causes. Falk appeals directly to the Thomistic framework: God is the primary cause of evolution, working through natural secondary causes without those causes being miraculous interventions detectable to science. This is not "deistic evolution" (God set things up and stepped away) but genuine theistic evolution in which God is the continuous ground of being of the entire process. The BioLogos response to the incoherence objection is precisely the Catholic response: the question of whether evolution is "guided" or "unguided" is a metaphysical question that science cannot answer, and claiming evolution is "unguided" at the scientific level is compatible with claiming it is "guided" at the theological level, because they are describing the same event from different levels of analysis.

Critical Assessment

This is a polemical volume — its purpose is to critique, not survey. It should be read alongside BioLogos responses for balance. Important note for Catholic readers: The Catholic tradition's approach to evolution — Aquinas's primary/secondary causation framework — is actually more sophisticated than what most of this book's contributors offer. The Catholic position can affirm real evolutionary processes as secondary causes while maintaining God as primary cause, without the incoherence the contributors allege, because it doesn't require God's "intervention" to be detectable at the secondary level. Many Catholic scientists and theologians (including the Vatican Observatory's staff) hold theistic evolution positions that don't concede the theological problems the contributors identify.

Connections to the Course

Week 6 (Catholicism and Science, primary/secondary causation, Lemaître), the Teilhard de Chardin lecture (Week 3). The Thomistic framework discussed in the Lemaître lecture is the Catholic answer to the incoherence objection that this book levels at theistic evolution.

Actionable Takeaways
  • The incoherence objection (you can't simultaneously affirm unguided evolution and say God directed it) is philosophically serious — theistic evolutionists need to answer it.
  • The Thomistic primary/secondary causation framework is the Catholic answer: God causes truly through natural causes, so the causation is genuinely "unguided" at the secondary level while being divinely directed at the primary level.
  • Read alongside BioLogos responses (Denis Alexander, Darrel Falk) and the Vatican Observatory's writings for the Catholic position.
Central Claim

Science and Christian faith not only can coexist — specific scientific findings in cosmology, biology, archaeology, and neuroscience actively support Judeo-Christian belief. This is a multi-author reference volume (45+ contributors) with a foreword by Meyer, organized as an encyclopedic treatment of science-faith questions from an intelligent design perspective.

Key Note on Authorship

This is not a Stephen C. Meyer solo work. Primary editors are William Dembski, Casey Luskin, and Joseph Holden. Meyer wrote the Foreword and is among the contributors. It is included here because it is frequently associated with Meyer and provides the broadest single-volume treatment of the ID research program.

Structure

Four sections: (1) Science and Faith — compatibility arguments, historical relationship, philosophical foundations; (2) Science and Design — the ID case across multiple domains; (3) Science and Evolution — critique of evolutionary theory; (4) Hard Questions — archaeology and Scripture, consciousness, fine-tuning, and specific apologetic issues. Contributors include Michael Behe, Douglas Axe, Jonathan Wells, Guillermo Gonzalez, Jay Richards, and others.

Critical Assessment

As a reference volume, the quality varies by chapter and contributor. Its chief value is breadth — it surveys more of the science-faith terrain than any of Meyer's individual books. It is stronger as a one-stop reference to find arguments on a specific topic than as a sustained argument to read cover to cover. The consistent ID-friendly perspective means alternative positions are often characterized rather than engaged at full strength. Useful as a reference alongside more mainstream science-faith resources (e.g., Ian Barbour's four-model framework, the Vatican Observatory's publications).

Connections to the Course

Week 6 (Catholicism and Science), and the Thaller/JRE discussion of science's limits. Best used as a reference when a specific science-faith question arises.

Actionable Takeaways
  • Use this as a reference, not a cover-to-cover read.
  • The section on historical relationships between science and Christianity (correcting the "warfare thesis") is particularly strong and accessible.
  • Note that the volume represents one coherent perspective (Discovery Institute / ID) — supplement with Ian Barbour or the Vatican Observatory for Catholic perspectives that differ on evolution.

Paul Copan

Central Claim

The God of the Old Testament — despite the portrait drawn by Richard Dawkins, Christopher Hitchens, and Sam Harris — is not an arbitrary, genocidal tyrant. The OT commands that trouble modern readers (Canaanite genocide, slavery, treatment of women, animal sacrifice) can be understood in their historical, literary, and theological context in ways that are morally defensible and in fact reveal a God more humane than his ancient contemporaries.

Supporting Arguments

Ancient Near Eastern Context: Israelite law compared to contemporary law codes (Hammurabi, Hittite, Assyrian) is significantly more humane — protections for slaves that no surrounding culture provided, limits on violence, provisions for the poor, rights for resident aliens. The charge that Mosaic law is barbaric ignores that it was operating in and gradually improving a Bronze Age context.

The Hyperbole Argument: The "utterly destroy" language in Joshua and Deuteronomy (herem warfare) is standard Ancient Near Eastern military rhetoric — the same hyperbolic victory language appears in Egyptian, Assyrian, and Moabite inscriptions and clearly doesn't mean literal killing of every person. The same texts that command destruction show Canaanites still living in the land afterward, confirming the commands weren't literally carried out as described.

Progressive Revelation: God accommodated his commands to the moral capacity of the Israelite people at a particular stage of their history. The slave laws, divorce provisions, and other concessions Moses permitted are not God's ideal — Jesus himself says this about divorce ("from the beginning it was not so" — Matthew 19:8). The trajectory of the biblical narrative points toward a higher standard consistently reached in the New Testament.

Specific Cases Examined: Slavery (debt-bondage vs. chattel slavery — very different institutions); treatment of women (Israelite women had more rights than most ANE contemporaries); the Canaanite "genocide" (directed at combatants in specific cities, not civilians throughout the region; Canaanites could and did leave).

Key Assumptions

The ANE context is the appropriate interpretive frame. Hyperbolic military rhetoric was a recognized convention, not deceptive. Progressive revelation is a coherent theological concept that allows for moral development across the testaments.

Counterarguments Addressed

Engages Dawkins (The God Delusion), Hitchens (God Is Not Great), and Harris (The End of Faith) directly. Also addresses the "divine command theory" objection (is something good merely because God commands it?) via the Euthyphro dilemma. Engages evangelical critics who think the hyperbole argument undermines biblical authority.

The Counterargument Case — From the Other Side

Richard Dawkins, The God Delusion, Chapter 2 (2006): Dawkins's "rogues' gallery" of OT divine actions — commanding genocide of the Canaanites, authorizing slavery, ordering the killing of Sabbath-breakers, demanding the execution of disobedient children — represents the sharpest popular-level statement of the case Copan is responding to. Dawkins's specific claim is not merely that the OT God behaved badly but that the behavior is logically inconsistent with the omnibenevolent God Christianity claims to worship — you cannot have both a genocide-commanding God and an all-loving God; the concepts contradict. Dawkins further argues that Copan-style responses ("context," "hyperbole," "progressive revelation") are motivated reasoning — the same hermeneutical moves would be condemned if applied to any other ancient text by religious apologists defending their own tradition. The honest response, Dawkins argues, is to admit the OT God is a moral monster and either abandon theism or admit the Bible is not morally authoritative.

Christopher Hitchens, God Is Not Great: How Religion Poisons Everything (2007): Hitchens's argument is structurally different from Dawkins's and harder for Copan to address: Hitchens is less interested in specific OT texts than in the general pattern of religiously authorized violence across history. Even if Copan successfully explains the Canaanite passages as hyperbole or context, Hitchens argues the historical record of actual Church behavior (Crusades, Inquisition, support for colonial violence, complicity in genocide) demonstrates that religious moral authority consistently produces moral catastrophe. The Bible's specific problems are downstream of the larger problem: that any tradition claiming divine authority for moral commands produces followers who will commit atrocities in the name of those commands. Copan's book-level response is largely to the text-critical arguments; the historical argument Hitchens makes is addressed more directly in Copan's later work but is never fully answered at the book level.

Eric Seibert, Disturbing Divine Behavior: Troubling Old Testament Images of God (2009): Seibert is a Christian OT scholar who argues Copan's approach — defending the OT texts as consistent with a good God — is both exegetically wrong and pastorally harmful. Seibert argues that the texts really do portray God commanding genocide, and that the appropriate Christian response is not to explain this away but to acknowledge that the human authors of Scripture got God wrong in these passages — that their portraits of God are distorted by their own cultural assumptions, military ideologies, and limited moral vision. On this view, Scripture is authoritative not because every portrait of God in it is accurate but because it traces a trajectory of progressive revelation in which the human understanding of God's character grows over time, culminating in Jesus, who is the definitive revelation of who God is. Seibert's critique of Copan is that the apologetic impulse to defend every biblical text produces a portrait of God that is less morally serious than the honest admission that some texts got him wrong.

Critical Assessment

This is one of the best popular-level responses to the new atheist critique of the OT. The ANE context argument is well-established in mainstream scholarship and not invented by ID apologists. The hyperbole argument is more contested — some scholars (both secular and Christian) find it reads too much into the texts, and some evangelical critics worry it opens the door to reading away other difficult passages. The slavery chapter requires careful reading: Copan's distinction between OT debt-bondage and American chattel slavery is legitimate but can feel like special pleading when the mistreatment of slaves in OT texts is examined in detail. Copan's overall approach is honest — he doesn't pretend the texts aren't challenging.

Connections to the Course

Week 7 (Antisemitism and the Church's historical relationship with Judaism, understanding why the OT world matters), Week 3 (Problem of Evil and theodicy). The Course's Week 2 lecture on Jewish roots of Catholic worship is better understood after reading Copan — the God who commanded the Canaanite wars is the same God whose liturgical patterns Catholics inherit.

Notable Quote
"The God of the Old Testament is often more gracious and less violent than his critics suggest — and the violence that does appear is set in a context that the new atheists systematically ignore."— Paul Copan, Is God a Moral Monster? (2011)
Actionable Takeaways
  • Learn the ANE context before defending the OT — knowing that Hammurabi's Code allows masters to kill slaves while Mosaic law does not changes the conversation.
  • The trajectory of Scripture matters as much as individual commands — the direction is consistently toward more dignity, more mercy, more universalism.
  • When someone cites the OT God as a reason not to believe, this is the book to have read.
Central Claim

The apparent tension between the "wrathful" God of the Old Testament and the "loving" God of the New Testament is a false dichotomy — both testaments present the same God with the same character. Readings that construct an OT monster and an NT softie misread both testaments. The OT is more gracious than critics admit; the NT is more demanding and judgment-oriented than progressive Christianity acknowledges.

Supporting Arguments

The OT's Gracious God: The OT is saturated with mercy, covenant love (hesed), patience, and forgiveness. God delays judgment for generations, pleads with Israel through the prophets, weeps over Jerusalem. The "wrath" passages exist alongside and are framed by abundant mercy. The God who commands the Canaanite wars is the same God who says "I take no pleasure in the death of the wicked" (Ezekiel 33:11).

The NT's Demanding God: Jesus speaks more about hell than any other figure in the NT. The book of Revelation presents the most graphic divine judgment imagery in all of Scripture. The NT's God is not softer — the demands are higher, the stakes are clearer (eternal rather than temporal consequences), and the judgment is more universal. Progressive Christianity's selective NT reading misses this consistently.

Against Marcionism: The heresy of Marcion (2nd century) — that the OT God is a lesser, malevolent deity different from the NT's loving Father — was the first major Christian heresy. Its modern equivalents (in progressive Christianity's dismissal of the OT God and in the new atheism's selective reading) make the same fundamental error.

NT Difficult Passages: Examines specific NT passages often cited as showing divine vindictiveness — the deaths of Ananias and Sapphira (Acts 5), eternal punishment imagery, the book of Revelation's violence, and Paul's "delivered to Satan" passages — and provides contextual interpretation of each.

Key Assumptions

The Bible speaks with a unified theological voice across testaments. Marcion's error is not just ancient — it shapes much contemporary reading. The same hermeneutical principles that work for OT difficult passages apply to NT ones.

Critical Assessment

A natural sequel to Is God a Moral Monster? that covers territory the first book left incomplete. Particularly valuable for the NT difficult passages chapter, which has no direct equivalent in evangelical apologetics. The section on eternal punishment will not satisfy universalists — Copan defends a traditional view of hell as eternal conscious punishment while acknowledging the tradition's inner tensions. Some reviewers find the second book more repetitive of the first than genuinely new; it is best read as a complement rather than a replacement. The Marcion chapter is the most historically important — the heresy it addresses is more widespread than most Catholics realize.

Connections to the Course

Week 3 (Death and Afterlife, hell), Week 7 (Antisemitism — the Marcionite roots of Christian anti-Judaism), the apophatic theology lecture (the God who exceeds our categories is also the God who judges).

Actionable Takeaways
  • Read Hosea 11 ("When Israel was a child I loved him...") alongside Revelation 19 (the rider on the white horse) — the same God is speaking in both. Understanding this prevents both sentimentality and severity.
  • Learn what Marcionism is and learn to recognize it — it's the most common theological error in progressive Christianity and in the culture at large.
  • Read this after Is God a Moral Monster? — they form a pair.
Central Claim

The biblical commands to "utterly destroy" the Canaanites (the herem warfare passages in Deuteronomy and Joshua) do not describe or mandate literal genocide of civilians, and can be understood — through careful attention to ANE literary conventions, internal biblical evidence, and moral philosophy — in ways consistent with a morally good God.

Supporting Arguments

The Hyperbole Argument (Primary): The "utterly destroy, leave nothing alive" language is standard Ancient Near Eastern military rhetoric — the same hyperbolic victory formula appears in the Merneptah Stele (Egyptian), Mesha Stele (Moabite), and Assyrian annals. In these texts it clearly doesn't mean literal killing of every person; it is a conventional claim of total victory. Applying this recognized literary convention to the Joshua texts explains why the same accounts that "utterly destroy" the Canaanites show them still very much present in subsequent chapters.

Internal Biblical Evidence: Joshua 10–11 use the herem formula repeatedly, but subsequent chapters (Judges 1) show Canaanites still living throughout the land. Either the herem commands were not carried out (in which case they were hyperbolic imperatives, not genocide commands) or the texts contradict themselves (the hyperbole reading is more coherent).

Who Was Actually Targeted: The commands target specific city-states and their military populations, not the entire Canaanite ethnic group throughout the region. Non-combatants could and did leave (Rahab's family; the Gibeonite treaty shows that surrender was an option). This is not ethnic cleansing of a people but military engagement with specific polities.

Philosophical Defense: Even granting a literal reading for the sake of argument, Copan and Flannagan argue that a specific divine command authorizing a particular historical action doesn't generate a general principle applicable to all times and places. God can authorize what would otherwise be impermissible — analogous to how a lawful authority can authorize actions that would be illegal without that authority (capital punishment, war).

Key Assumptions

ANE hyperbolic conventions apply to these texts. The internal biblical evidence (Canaanites still present after the "destruction") is meant to be read at face value rather than as a contradiction. Divine command theory has a coherent philosophical defense.

Counterarguments Addressed

Engages philosopher Wes Morriston's objections to divine command theory. Addresses Hector Avalos's secular critique. Engages evangelical critics who argue the hyperbole reading undermines biblical inerrancy. Also addresses the "if God commanded it, isn't it by definition good?" (Euthyphro) objection with Flannagan's modified divine command theory.

The Counterargument Case — From the Other Side

Wes Morriston, "Did God Command Genocide? A Challenge to the Biblical Inerrantist," Philosophia Christi (2009): Morriston's philosophical objection is the most technically rigorous challenge to Copan and Flannagan's divine command theory defense. His argument: even granting that God could authorize actions normally impermissible, the specific claim — that God commanded the killing of Canaanite infants and children — cannot be morally justified by any framework that also claims God is perfectly good. If we say "God commanded it, therefore it was just," we have made God's commands the definition of justice rather than a consequence of it, which means justice has no content independent of God's will — making the claim "God is just" tautologically empty. If we say "God commanded it because it was just," we must be able to give an independent account of why the killing of infants is just — which Copan and Flannagan cannot do without invoking the very moral framework they claim God's command supersedes. Morriston argues the divine command defense is philosophically incoherent: it either empties justice of content or requires a moral justification for infant killing that doesn't exist.

Hector Avalos, The End of Biblical Studies (2007): Avalos, a secular biblical scholar, argues from a different direction: that the entire project of making the Bible morally relevant to contemporary life is misguided, because the Bible's moral framework is so thoroughly embedded in ancient Near Eastern assumptions (covenant obligation, collective punishment, ethnic election) that no amount of contextualizing or reinterpreting makes it a suitable guide for modern ethics. On the genocide passages specifically, Avalos argues the hyperbole reading is not supported by the biblical authors' own self-understanding — they believed they were recording divine commands that were literally obeyed. The later historiographical reflection that shows Canaanites still in the land (Judges 1) is not evidence that Joshua's commands were figurative; it is evidence that Israel failed to carry out what it believed was a genuine divine mandate. Avalos's challenge to Copan is fundamentally about authorial intent: the texts mean what their authors intended, and their authors intended a literal genocide command.

The Moral Intuition Problem: Perhaps the deepest objection Copan and Flannagan face — articulated across multiple critics — is what philosophers call the "moral intuition problem." Both authors ultimately appeal to the claim that our moral intuitions (that killing innocent children is wrong) can be overridden by divine command. But our moral intuitions are also the source of our belief that God is good — if they're reliable enough to ground theism, they're reliable enough to condemn the Canaanite passages as morally monstrous. C.S. Lewis himself recognized this problem: if God commanded something our moral intuitions recognize as evil, we would have more reason to doubt the revelation than to abandon the intuition. Some Christian philosophers (including Lewis and Alvin Plantinga) have argued this makes divine command theory of the Copan/Flannagan variety self-undermining.

Critical Assessment

The most academically rigorous of the three Copan volumes — co-authored with philosopher Matthew Flannagan, whose contributions give the philosophical sections significantly more depth than Is God a Moral Monster? The hyperbole argument is supported by significant ANE scholarship (Nicholas Wolterstorff, John Goldingay, and others outside the apologetics world). Critics on the secular left find the argument apologetically motivated. Critics on the evangelical right worry it concedes too much to historical-critical methodology. The most honest assessment: the hyperbole reading is a genuine scholarly position supported by mainstream ANE scholarship, not an apologetic invention — but it is not the only scholarly reading, and the philosophical divine command section is more controversial than the literary analysis section.

Connections to the Course

Week 7 (Antisemitism and the Church's relationship with Judaism — why Christians need to understand the OT on its own terms), Week 3 (Problem of Evil). The most focused and scholarly treatment of the single most common intellectual objection to the OT in the course reading list.

Notable Quote
"The herem texts, read in their literary and historical context, look much less like commands to exterminate civilians and much more like the hyperbolic victory rhetoric found throughout the ancient Near East — rhetoric whose purpose was proclamation of divine triumph, not literal description of killing."— Paul Copan and Matthew Flannagan, Did God Really Command Genocide? (2014)
Actionable Takeaways
  • Learn the Merneptah Stele and the Mesha Stele — they provide the ANE parallel texts that make the hyperbole argument concrete rather than speculative.
  • The question "did the Canaanites still live in the land after the 'destruction'?" is the single most important question to ask when reading Joshua — the text itself answers it.
  • Read this before any serious apologetic conversation about the OT — it's the most specific and scholarly available response to the most specific and serious objection.
Appendix G · Reference

History of Theological Movements

Two thousand years of Christian intellectual history — the major movements, heresies, schools, and revolutions that shaped Catholic theology from the Apostolic era to the age of artificial intelligence. Click any era to expand it.

Color coding: ORTHODOX movements affirmed as Catholic teaching   HETERODOX condemned or rejected   PROTESTANT Reformation-era traditions   MODERN post-Enlightenment movements

I

Apostolic & Patristic Era · 30–750 AD

720 years

Gnosticism · c. 80–300 AD · HETERODOX

Key figures: Valentinus, Marcion, Basilides. Core claim: The material world was created by an inferior, malevolent deity (the Demiurge — identified with the OT God), while the true God is a purely spiritual being of pure light. Christ came to rescue spiritual souls from entrapment in matter — his body was illusory (Docetism). Salvation is achieved through secret knowledge (gnosis), not faith or sacraments. What happened: Condemned by Irenaeus (Against Heresies, c. 180), Tertullian, and Origen. But Gnosticism has never died — its basic instincts (spirit good / matter bad; secret knowledge for the elite; the OT God is different from the NT God) recur in every century. Its contemporary heirs include some New Age spirituality, certain progressive Christian dismissals of the OT, and academic "Gnostic Gospel" scholarship (Elaine Pagels).

Marcionism · c. 140–160 AD · HETERODOX

Key figure: Marcion of Sinope. Core claim: The OT God (wrathful, tribal, violent) and the NT God (loving, universal, gracious) are literally different beings. Christianity should be completely separated from Judaism. Marcion produced the first Christian canon — a shortened Luke and ten Pauline letters with all OT references removed. What happened: Excommunicated 144 AD. Paradoxically, his attempt to remove the OT from Christianity provoked the Church to formally define the canon to include it — Marcionism caused the emergence of the biblical canon as a defined list. Condemned definitively but its cultural residue persists in every "loving NT God vs. angry OT God" contrast.

Montanism · c. 155–220 AD · HETERODOX

Key figure: Montanus; later joined by Tertullian. Core claim: The age of prophecy was not over — the Holy Spirit continued to speak new revelation through charismatic prophets (specifically Montanus and two female prophetesses, Prisca and Maximilla), whose utterances superseded apostolic tradition. Strict asceticism, imminent expectation of the Second Coming. What happened: Condemned for claiming ongoing revelation that superseded Scripture. But Montanism anticipated the tension between institutional and charismatic Christianity that surfaces again in every century — most recently in the Catholic Charismatic Renewal.

Arianism · c. 318–381 AD · HETERODOX

Key figure: Arius of Alexandria. Core claim: The Son of God is the highest of all created beings — subordinate to, and different in essence from, the Father. "There was a time when the Son was not." Christ is divine in a secondary sense; only the Father is truly God. What happened: Condemned at the Council of Nicaea (325 AD) and again at Constantinople (381 AD). The Nicene Creed's "consubstantial with the Father" (homoousios) was written specifically to exclude Arianism. Despite condemnation, Arianism spread through the Germanic tribes who converted to Christianity — the Visigoths, Vandals, Ostrogoths, and Lombards were all Arian Christians for centuries. Its modern heirs: Jehovah's Witnesses hold an essentially Arian Christology.

Donatism · c. 311–411 AD · HETERODOX

Key figure: Donatus Magnus of Carthage. Core claim: The validity of sacraments depends on the personal holiness of the minister — a priest who had apostasized during the Diocletianic persecution could not validly baptize or ordain. The Church must be a pure community of saints, not a mixed body of saints and sinners. What happened: Condemned by Councils and opposed by Augustine, who developed the Catholic doctrine of ex opere operato — sacraments are valid by virtue of the act itself, not the holiness of the minister. Augustine's anti-Donatist theology permanently shaped Catholic sacramental theology. The underlying question (visible purity vs. institutional continuity) never goes away.

Pelagianism · c. 400–418 AD · HETERODOX

Key figure: Pelagius (British monk in Rome). Core claim: Human beings are born without original sin — capable of choosing good by free will without any prior divine grace. Sin is a matter of bad habits, not a damaged nature. Christ's redemption is primarily moral example and forgiveness, not ontological healing of human nature. What happened: Condemned at the Council of Carthage (418 AD) and the Council of Ephesus (431 AD), largely through Augustine's counter-arguments. Augustine's alternative (original sin, prevenient grace, predestination) shaped Western theology for a millennium but created its own problems — especially regarding predestination, which Luther and Calvin pushed to their logical extreme. Semi-Pelagianism (humans can initiate the movement toward God without grace) was also condemned at the Second Council of Orange (529 AD). The Pelagian instinct — that human effort is sufficient — is arguably the default position of most people's practical religion.

Nestorianism · c. 428–431 AD · HETERODOX

Key figure: Nestorius, Patriarch of Constantinople. Core claim: Christ is two persons (divine and human) who are morally united but not ontologically one. Mary is the mother of the human person Christ, not the mother of God — she should be called Christotokos (God-bearer of Christ), not Theotokos (God-bearer). What happened: Condemned at the Council of Ephesus (431 AD), which defined Mary as Theotokos. The Church of the East (Assyrian Church) did not accept Ephesus and maintained what it considered an orthodox Christology — it survives to this day as one of the oldest continuous Christian communities.

Miaphysitism / Oriental Orthodoxy · c. 451 AD · ORTHODOX (separate communion)

Key figures: Dioscorus of Alexandria, Timothy II. Core claim: Christ has one nature (mia physis) — not one that is purely divine (Monophysitism, condemned) but one that is fully divine and fully human united without division, confusion, or alteration. What happened: The Council of Chalcedon (451 AD) defined Christ as "one person in two natures." The Egyptian Coptic, Ethiopian, Armenian, and Syriac churches rejected Chalcedon's two-natures formula as Nestorian and maintained the miaphysite position. This split has never been fully healed, though Catholic-Oriental Orthodox dialogue has concluded that the condemnations were largely based on terminological misunderstanding — both sides confessed the same faith in different vocabulary.

II

Medieval Era · 750–1517

770 years

Iconoclasm · 726–843 AD · HETERODOX

Key figures: Emperor Leo III (Byzantine), later Constantine V. Core claim: The veneration of religious images (icons) is idolatry. All images of Christ and the saints should be destroyed. What happened: Condemned by the Second Council of Nicaea (787 AD), which defined the distinction between latria (worship due to God alone) and proskynesis/dulia (veneration appropriate for holy persons and objects). Iconoclasm was restored under Emperor Leo V (815 AD) and finally defeated in 843 AD — the "Triumph of Orthodoxy" still celebrated annually in the Eastern Church. The theological principle established: images of the Incarnate Christ are not idols but proclamations of the Incarnation — to reject the icon is to reject the claim that God truly became visible.

Carolingian Scholasticism · c. 800–1050 AD · ORTHODOX

Key figures: John Scotus Eriugena, Alcuin of York, Anselm of Canterbury. Core claim: Faith seeks understanding (fides quaerens intellectum — Anselm). Reason and faith are not in conflict; theology can and should be pursued with rigorous philosophical method. Anselm's ontological argument for God's existence (the greatest conceivable being must exist in reality as well as in the mind). What it produced: The intellectual foundation for High Scholasticism and Thomism — the confidence that reason and faith are complementary rather than adversarial.

High Scholasticism / Thomism · c. 1200–1300 AD · ORTHODOX

Key figures: Thomas Aquinas (Dominican), Bonaventure (Franciscan), Albert the Great. Core claim: Aristotelian philosophy — recovered through Arabic translations — provides the best conceptual framework for articulating Christian theology. Grace does not destroy nature but perfects it. The Five Ways demonstrate God's existence by reason. Transubstantiation can be articulated using Aristotelian substance/accident metaphysics. The natural law can be known by reason independent of revelation. What it produced: The Summa Theologiae — the most comprehensive theological synthesis in Western history. Thomism became the official philosophical method of the Catholic Church (formally endorsed at Vatican I, reaffirmed at Vatican II). Its influence on every major Catholic development from canon law to bioethics to the theology of the sacraments is incalculable.

Nominalism · c. 1300–1500 AD · MODERN PRECURSOR

Key figures: William of Ockham, John Duns Scotus (earlier), Gabriel Biel. Core claim: Universal concepts (humanity, justice, goodness) are not real things — they are names (nomina) applied to collections of particular individuals. Only particular, concrete things exist. God's will is not constrained by any prior rational nature — God could have commanded the opposite of what he did command, and it would be good because he commanded it. What it produced: Voluntarism in theology (God's will is prior to God's reason) — which led directly to Luther's emphasis on God's absolute sovereignty and arbitrary grace, and Calvin's double predestination. Ockham's "razor" (don't multiply entities beyond necessity) shaped the empiricist tradition that produced modern science. Nominalism's separation of faith and reason paved the way for the Reformation and for secular modernity simultaneously.

Rhineland Mysticism · c. 1280–1380 AD · ORTHODOX (mostly)

Key figures: Meister Eckhart, Johannes Tauler, Henry Suso, Jan van Ruusbroec. Core claim: The soul's union with God is achievable in this life through interior detachment (Gelassenheit — letting go), the "spark of the soul" (Seelenfunklein) that is never fully separated from God, and apophatic contemplation that goes beyond all concepts and images. What happened: Eckhart's most extreme formulations were condemned by John XXII (1329) — but his influence through Tauler and Suso spread across all of Europe and ultimately into the Reformation (Luther read Tauler). The Rhineland mystics represent the Catholic tradition's most radical and most honest engagement with divine incomprehensibility. Their influence on the apophatic theology in Week 5 of this course is direct.

Conciliarism · c. 1378–1460 AD · HETERODOX

Key figures: Jean Gerson, Marsilio of Padua, Nicholas of Cusa (early). Core claim: A General Council of the Church has authority superior to the Pope — the Pope is accountable to the Council, not the other way around. Arising from the Western Schism (1378–1417, when there were two and briefly three competing popes), conciliarism proposed a constitutional rather than monarchical model of Church governance. What happened: Condemned at Vatican I (1870) with the definition of papal infallibility. But conciliarist instincts never disappeared — they resurface in every debate about synodality, the proper relationship between bishops and the Pope, and the current Synod on Synodality under Francis.

III

Reformation Era · 1517–1700

183 years

Lutheran Reformation · 1517 onward · PROTESTANT

Key figures: Martin Luther, Philip Melanchthon. Core claims: Sola fide (justification by faith alone), sola scriptura (Scripture alone as the final authority), sola gratia (grace alone). The Catholic Church had obscured the Gospel through institutional accretions, works-righteousness, indulgences, and clerical corruption. Luther retained infant baptism, real presence in the Eucharist (consubstantiation — Christ's body truly present alongside the bread, not instead of it), and episcopal structure in some forms. What it produced: The permanent fracture of Western Christianity. The Council of Trent (1545–1563) was convened largely in response. The 1999 Joint Declaration on Justification and the 2016 Lund commemoration represent the partial reconciliation of five centuries later.

Reformed / Calvinist Theology · 1530s onward · PROTESTANT

Key figures: John Calvin, Ulrich Zwingli, Heinrich Bullinger, John Knox. Core claims: Double predestination — God elected some for salvation and others for damnation from eternity before creation. The Eucharist is a memorial, not a sacrifice — Christ is spiritually (not physically) present. The Church must be reformed according to Scripture alone, not tradition. Total depravity of human nature after the Fall — no capacity for good without divine election. What it produced: Presbyterian and Reformed churches; Puritanism; eventually the democratic political theology underlying the American founding. Its doctrine of double predestination is the sharpest theological challenge to the Catholic doctrine of universal salvific will.

The Catholic Counter-Reformation · 1545–1648 · ORTHODOX

Key figures: Ignatius of Loyola, Charles Borromeo, Teresa of Ávila, John of the Cross, Robert Bellarmine. Key institution: The Council of Trent (1545–1563). Core claims: Justification requires cooperation between grace and free will (against Lutheran monergism). Both Scripture and Tradition are sources of revelation (against sola scriptura). The seven sacraments are genuine means of grace. Transubstantiation is the correct account of the Eucharist. The Vulgate is the authentic biblical text. Seminary education and clerical discipline must be reformed. What it produced: The Jesuits (the most important missionary and intellectual order in modern Catholic history), the reformed Carmelites (Teresa and John of the Cross), systematic Catholic apologetics, and the shape of Catholicism that prevailed until Vatican II.

Jansenism · 1640–1713 · HETERODOX

Key figures: Cornelius Jansen, Antoine Arnauld, Blaise Pascal. Core claim: A rigorous Augustinianism: divine grace is irresistible for the elect; frequent reception of Communion is inappropriate for ordinary sinners who should first achieve a high degree of contrition; the moral theology of the Jesuits (especially probabilism — the idea that a less certain moral opinion can justify an action) is laxist and corrupting. What happened: Condemned by Clement XI's bull Unigenitus (1713). But Jansenism's cultural influence was enormous — its severity and rigorism shaped French Catholicism and Irish Catholicism (imported via Irish seminaries in France) for generations. The scrupulosity and fear-based spirituality that characterized pre-Vatican II Catholic practice in many countries is partially a Jansenist inheritance.

Quietism · 1670s–1690s · HETERODOX

Key figures: Miguel de Molinos, Madame Guyon, Archbishop Fénelon. Core claim: Perfect union with God requires the annihilation of human will and activity — even the desire for salvation, virtue, and avoiding sin should be surrendered. In pure contemplation, the soul becomes entirely passive and God acts through it without any human cooperation. What happened: Condemned by Innocent XI (1687) and Clement XI (1699). But Quietism's condemnation prompted the Church to articulate more carefully the difference between apophatic contemplation (legitimate) and passivism that eliminates human moral agency (illegitimate). The mystical tradition after Quietism was always under scrutiny — contributing to the Church's historical suspicion of contemplative practice.

IV

Enlightenment & Modern Era · 1700–1960

260 years

Deism · c. 1680–1800 · MODERN

Key figures: John Locke, Voltaire, Thomas Jefferson, Thomas Paine. Core claim: God exists and created the universe (the cosmological and design arguments justify this), but does not intervene in it — no miracles, no revelation, no incarnation. Religion's job is to articulate the natural moral law, not supernatural redemption. Christianity should be stripped of its miraculous and doctrinal content, retaining only rational ethics. What it produced: The intellectual background of the French Revolution and the American founding. Deism's cultural residue is enormous — the default secular theism of much of the Western world ("I believe in God but not organized religion") is functionally Deist.

Liberal Protestantism / Historical-Critical Method · c. 1780–1920 · MODERN

Key figures: Friedrich Schleiermacher, Albrecht Ritschl, Adolf von Harnack, David Friedrich Strauss. Core claim: Christianity must be reinterpreted in light of Enlightenment reason and historical science. The essence of Christianity is not doctrine but religious experience (Schleiermacher) or ethical teaching (Harnack: "the Fatherhood of God and the Brotherhood of Man"). The historical Jesus was a moral teacher; the Christ of dogma is a later ecclesiastical construction. Miracles are premodern mythology that modern people cannot accept. What it produced: The historical-critical method for reading Scripture (now standard in all Catholic biblical scholarship, including Bergsma and Pitre). Also produced the reaction against it — neo-orthodoxy (Barth) and fundamentalism. The Modernist Crisis in Catholicism (1907) was Rome's specific response to Catholic scholars adopting these methods.

Catholic Modernism · c. 1890–1910 · HETERODOX

Key figures: Alfred Loisy, George Tyrrell, Friedrich von Hügel. Core claim: Catholic theology must engage modern historical scholarship honestly — the Bible is historically conditioned, dogmas develop over time, and religious experience is foundational. See Week 1's full treatment of the Modernist Crisis and its rehabilitation by Vatican II.

Neo-Scholasticism / Neo-Thomism · c. 1879–1960 · ORTHODOX

Key figures: Jacques Maritain, Étienne Gilson, Reginald Garrigou-Lagrange. Initiated by: Leo XIII's encyclical Aeterni Patris (1879), mandating the study of Thomas Aquinas as the basis of Catholic philosophy and theology. Core claim: The Thomistic synthesis — Aristotelian metaphysics integrated with Christian revelation — remains the best framework for Catholic intellectual life and the best defense against the errors of both liberalism and Marxism. What it produced: The philosophical framework underlying the anti-Modernist response, the Catechism of 1992, Catholic social teaching, and most 20th-century Catholic bioethics. Still the dominant philosophical method in Catholic seminaries worldwide.

Neo-Orthodoxy / Dialectical Theology · c. 1919–1960 · PROTESTANT

Key figures: Karl Barth, Emil Brunner, Reinhold Niebuhr. Core claim: A revolt against Liberal Protestantism from within Protestantism. God is "wholly other" — not accessible through human reason, religious experience, or cultural progress. The Word of God (Scripture and the proclaimed Gospel) breaks into human history from outside, judging all human pretension including religious pretension. Sin is not a moral defect to be improved but a fundamental disorder of the will that only grace can address. What it produced: The most rigorous Protestant theological system of the 20th century. Barth's Church Dogmatics (14 volumes) is the most ambitious Protestant systematic theology ever written. Catholic-Barth dialogue was important for Vatican II's approach to revelation (Dei Verbum).

Nouvelle Théologie (New Theology) · c. 1930–1965 · ORTHODOX (rehabilitated)

Key figures: Henri de Lubac, Yves Congar, Hans Urs von Balthasar, Karl Rahner, Marie-Dominique Chenu. Core claim: Neo-Scholasticism had become a rigid, ahistorical system that needed to be renewed by returning to the sources — the Church Fathers, the full biblical tradition, and liturgical theology. The separation of nature and grace (Neo-Scholastic "extrinsicism") was wrong: the human person has a natural desire for the supernatural, so grace is not an alien addition to nature but nature's own deepest fulfillment. What happened: Suppressed by Pius XII's encyclical Humani Generis (1950), which ordered de Lubac's books withdrawn. Then Vatican II made their ideas its own. De Lubac, Congar, and Balthasar were made cardinals. Rahner became the most influential Catholic theologian of the 20th century. The classic case of the tradition suppressing and then absorbing its own best thinkers (see Week 1).

Process Theology · c. 1925–present · MODERN

Key figures: Alfred North Whitehead, Charles Hartshorne, John Cobb Jr. Core claim: God is not the immutable, impassible, omnipotent creator of classical theism but is himself in process — affected by the world, growing in relation to it, persuading rather than coercing creatures. God is the "fellow sufferer who understands." Classical theism's God is incompatible with genuine creaturely freedom and genuine divine love. What it produced: A significant influence on liberal Protestant theology and a minor Catholic following. The Catholic mainstream has rejected process theology's God as incompatible with the tradition's account of divine simplicity, immutability, and omnipotence — but the theodicy problem it addresses (how an omnipotent God can coexist with evil) remains genuinely difficult.

V

Contemporary Era · 1960–Present

65 years and counting

Vatican II Theology · 1962–1965 and beyond · ORTHODOX

Key documents: Lumen Gentium (Church), Dei Verbum (Revelation), Gaudium et Spes (Church in the Modern World), Dignitatis Humanae (Religious Liberty), Nostra Aetate (Non-Christian Religions). Key claim: The Church must read the "signs of the times" and engage the modern world with the resources of the tradition, not simply condemn it. Religious liberty is a genuine right. Jews are not collectively guilty for the death of Christ. The liturgy should be reformed to enable active participation. What it produced: The most significant development in Catholic theology since Trent. Also produced the "hermeneutic wars" — the debate over whether Vatican II represents continuity with or rupture from the prior tradition, which remains the defining theological controversy in Catholicism today.

Liberation Theology · c. 1968–1990s · MODERN (partially absorbed)

Key figures: Gustavo Gutiérrez, Leonardo Boff, Jon Sobrino, Oscar Romero (as practitioner). Core claim: Theology must begin from the perspective of the poor — "the preferential option for the poor" is not optional but constitutive of the Gospel. See Week 8 for full treatment. The Marxist analytical framework was condemned by the CDF (Ratzinger, 1984); the core option for the poor was absorbed into mainstream CST and became the theological foundation of Francis's pontificate.

Death of God Theology · c. 1961–1968 · HETERODOX

Key figures: Thomas J.J. Altizer, William Hamilton, Paul van Buren. Core claim: In the modern secular world, "God" is no longer a live option for intellectually honest people — theology must be radically reconceived without the concept of God, or with the concept of God's self-emptying death in Christ (Altizer). Harvey Cox's companion work The Secular City (1965) was more moderate — the secular city represents Christianity's own desacralizing of the world, and secular life is the proper context for Christian engagement. What happened: A brief academic movement with almost no lasting influence except as a marker of 1960s theological crisis. The subsequent four decades of global religious vitality made "Death of God" theology look historically premature.

Feminist Theology · c. 1970–present · MODERN

Key figures: Elisabeth Schüssler Fiorenza, Rosemary Radford Ruether, Elizabeth Johnson. Core claim: The Christian tradition has been systematically shaped by patriarchal assumptions that distort the Gospel — the subordination of women in ecclesial life, the exclusive use of male language for God, the exclusion of women's experience from theological reflection. Genuine Christian theology requires feminist reconstruction. Within Catholicism, the debate centers specifically on women's ordination (forbidden by John Paul II's Ordinatio Sacerdotalis, 1994) and Trinitarian language. What it produced: Significant permanent contributions to biblical scholarship, historical recovery of women's roles in early Christianity, and the theological legitimization of women's experience as a theological locus. The ordination question remains the most divisive institutional question in contemporary Catholicism.

Intelligent Design Movement · c. 1991–present · MODERN

Key figures: Philip Johnson, Michael Behe, William Dembski, Stephen Meyer, Ann Gauger. Institutional base: Discovery Institute (Seattle). Core claim: Certain features of living organisms — irreducible complexity in molecular machines (Behe), specified complexity in DNA (Meyer) — cannot be adequately explained by Neo-Darwinian mechanisms and constitute positive evidence for intelligent design. ID is a scientific hypothesis, not a religious claim. What happened: The Kitzmiller v. Dover trial (2005) concluded that ID is not science and cannot be taught in public schools as a scientific alternative to evolution. The scientific mainstream has not accepted ID as a legitimate research program. The Catholic Church has not endorsed ID — the Vatican Observatory and most Catholic biologists accept evolutionary biology. ID's influence has been primarily in apologetics and popular-level science-faith discussions, not in peer-reviewed biology.

Evolutionary Creationism / BioLogos · c. 2006–present · MODERN

Key figures: Francis Collins, Darrel Falk, Dennis Venema, N.T. Wright (sympathizer). Institutional base: BioLogos Foundation (founded 2007). Core claim: Evolutionary biology and Christian faith are fully compatible — God created through an evolutionary process, and the genome is "the language of God." The scientific evidence for evolution is overwhelming and Christians should embrace it. BioLogos specifically represents an evangelical Protestant position; the Catholic equivalent is simply called "theistic evolution" and is the dominant position among Catholic biologists and the Vatican Observatory. Relationship to ID: BioLogos and the Discovery Institute are engaged in a significant ongoing debate — essentially the same debate addressed in Meyer's Theistic Evolution book (Appendix F of this course).

New Atheism · c. 2004–2015 · MODERN

Key figures: Richard Dawkins, Christopher Hitchens, Sam Harris, Daniel Dennett ("the Four Horsemen"). Core claim: Religion is not merely false but harmful — it poisons everything (Hitchens), provides evolutionary comfort for claims that deserve no comfort (Dawkins), makes moderate religion complicit in extremism (Harris), and is a set of memes that replicate regardless of their truth (Dennett). Aggressive public advocacy for atheism is not merely permitted but morally required. What it produced: A massive popular conversation about religion and evidence in the 2000s–2010s. Dawkins's The God Delusion (2006) sold over 3 million copies. The New Atheists are the primary audience for Copan's three books in Appendix F. Their influence has declined since its 2006–2010 peak — partly because their arguments were answered (Copan, Meyer, Hart, Ward), partly because their tone produced backlash, and partly because the "secularization thesis" (that modernity produces atheism) has proven empirically false globally.

Catholic Integralism · c. 2012–present · MODERN

Key figures: Edmund Waldstein OCist., Patrick Deneen, Sohrab Ahmari, Pater Edmund Waldstein. Core claim: The political order should be ordered toward the supernatural end of humanity and therefore toward the Church. Liberal democracy's claim to neutrality on questions of the good life is itself a substantive theological position — and a false one. See Week 8 for full treatment.

Synodality · c. 2021–present · ORTHODOX (developing)

Institutional base: Synod on Synodality (2021–2024), Pope Francis. Core claim: The Church's decision-making should be more consultative, including the voices of laypeople, women, and marginalized groups in theological discernment — not just bishops. Synodality does not replace the hierarchical structure but enriches it. What happened: The Synod on Synodality (the most extensive consultative process in Catholic history) produced its final document in 2024. The debate about what synodality actually means — consultation vs. governance, pastoral accompaniment vs. doctrinal change — continues at every level of the Church and will shape the post-Francis pontificate.

AI Ethics in Catholic Theology · 2023–present · ORTHODOX (emerging)

Key documents: Laudato Si' §§102–114 (technocratic paradigm, 2015); Laudate Deum (2023); Antiqua et Nova (Dicastery for the Doctrine of the Faith, January 2025); Magnifica Humanitas (Leo XIV, May 2026). Core claim: Artificial intelligence is not morally neutral — it takes on the characteristics of those who devise, finance, regulate, and use it. The Church must apply the principles of Catholic Social Teaching (human dignity, common good, subsidiarity, solidarity) to the governance of AI. The danger is not robots but power — the concentration of AI capability in private transnational entities accountable to no one. The theological category is not science vs. faith but Babel vs. Nehemiah: the city built on self-assertion vs. the city built on relationship, responsibility, and God. The movement is at its beginning; this is the defining theological engagement of the current generation.

Appendix H · Reference

Vatican II Documents

All four major Vatican II document types in summary form, organized by authority: the two Dogmatic Constitutions (highest authority), the Pastoral Constitution, and the three Declarations. Also includes Vatican I's two Dogmatic Constitutions for context.

A Dogmatic Constitution addresses matters of faith and doctrine with the full weight of an Ecumenical Council and the Pope acting together. Below: chapter-by-chapter summaries, key canons, historical context, and significance for the tradition. Documents are presented in chronological order.

Promulgated by: Pius IX · Vote: 667 in favor, 0 against, 55 abstentions · Structure: Preamble + 4 Chapters + Canons · Full Latin text: vatican.va

Historical Context: The Enlightenment had produced a range of positions hostile to Catholic faith: materialism (only matter exists), pantheism (the world is God), rationalism (human reason is the sole arbiter of truth, revelation impossible), deism (God exists but does not intervene), and fideism (the opposite error — faith is independent of reason entirely). Dei Filius was the Council's systematic response, defining the Catholic position on God, revelation, faith, and the relationship of faith and reason against all these errors simultaneously. It is not a polemical document — it defines what the Catholic Church actually believes with precision and then appends the canons condemning the opposing positions.

Preamble

§The Son of God, founder of the Church, promised his assistance to it until the end of time. The Council is convened not to define new doctrine but to condemn the errors of the age and restore the clarity of Catholic teaching, invoking the light of the Holy Spirit.

Chapter I — God, Creator of All Things

1The one true God is eternal, infinite in every perfection — omnipotent, omniscient, omnipresent, personal, and absolutely distinct from the world. He is not the world, not part of the world, and not the world's ground of being in a pantheist sense.
2God created the world freely, from nothing, without any compulsion or necessity — motivated only by his goodness and his will to manifest that goodness. Creation is an act of free divine generosity, not a necessary emanation or overflow of divine being.
3God can be known with certainty by the natural light of human reason from the things that are made — a direct affirmation of the cosmological and teleological arguments and a rejection of Kantian skepticism about metaphysical knowledge. The Council here affirms what Paul states in Romans 1:20: "invisible realities... have been clearly seen, being understood from what has been made."

Key Canon: "If anyone shall say that the one true God, our Creator and Lord, cannot be known with certainty by the natural light of human reason by means of the things that have been made — let him be anathema."

Chapter II — Revelation

1Although God can be known by reason, supernatural revelation was nonetheless necessary — because the truths about God and humanity's relationship to him that reason can reach are difficult, uncertain, accessible only to the few, and slow in coming. God in his goodness revealed these truths so that they might be available to all, with certainty, from the beginning.
2Beyond what reason can reach, revelation communicates truths that exceed the capacity of natural reason entirely — the mysteries of the Trinity, the Incarnation, the supernatural destiny of human persons. These can only be known through divine revelation and accepted through faith.
3This revelation, given to humanity through the prophets and finally fully through the Son, is preserved and transmitted by the Catholic Church, is contained in written Scripture and unwritten Tradition, and is to be received with divine and Catholic faith.

Key Canon: "If anyone shall say that divine revelation cannot be made credible by external signs, and that therefore men ought to be moved to faith only by each one's internal experience or private inspiration — let him be anathema."

Chapter III — Faith

1Faith is a supernatural virtue by which we believe what God has revealed to be true — not because we have independently verified it by natural reason, but because of the authority of God who reveals it, who can neither deceive nor be deceived. Faith is therefore not opinion, not feeling, not a working hypothesis — it is an assent of the intellect moved by grace to what God has revealed.
2Faith is free — no one is coerced by God or by the Church to believe. But faith is also rational — the credibility of revelation is established by external signs: miracles, fulfilled prophecy, the remarkable history of the Church. These signs are accessible to natural reason and make faith "in accordance with reason," not contrary to it.
3Faith, once given, must be held firmly and perseveringly. To have once believed and then knowingly abandoned the faith out of intellectual pride or moral failure is gravely sinful, not an exercise of intellectual honesty.

Key Canon: "If anyone shall say that human reason is so independent that faith cannot be demanded of it by God — let him be anathema."

Chapter IV — Faith and Reason

1Faith and reason are not in contradiction — both come from God. God cannot contradict himself. Apparent contradictions between a true result of reason and a revealed truth indicate either that the reasoning has gone wrong or that the revealed truth has been misunderstood. The resolution is always found at the level of correct method, not by subordinating one to the other.
2While faith and reason are distinct — their objects, methods, and principles are different — they are not separate or hostile. Reason can demonstrate the preambles of faith (God's existence, the credibility of revelation), and faith can protect reason from error by providing truths that correct the conclusions of corrupted reason.
3The Church does not oppose the progress of the human sciences — it does not forbid them to employ their own methods within their own proper sphere. But it resists the claim that science can pronounce on questions outside its competence: the supernatural, the existence of God, the soul, the final destiny of persons. These belong to theology, not to natural science.
4The Church condemns two equal and opposite errors: rationalism (which makes reason the arbiter of faith, subjecting revelation to natural verification) and fideism (which makes faith independent of reason, rejecting the natural knowledge of God and the rational defensibility of faith). Both errors corrupt the relationship between nature and grace.

Key Canon: "If anyone shall say that it is possible that, as knowledge advances, a meaning should sometimes be attributed to dogmas proposed by the Church different from that which the Church has understood and understands — let him be anathema." (The famous anti-evolution-of-dogma canon — which generated enormous controversy in the 20th century as the Nouvelle Théologie engaged the question of doctrinal development.)

Why Dei Filius Matters

This is the Catholic Church's most precise formal statement on the relationship of faith and reason — the metaphysical foundation of the entire Week 6 content on Catholicism and science. Stephen Meyer's argument (Appendix F) that science and theism are compatible is a specific application of the framework Dei Filius defines. The document also establishes the Catholic position that God's existence is genuinely knowable by natural reason — which is why the Church can engage Aquinas's Five Ways as rational demonstrations rather than leaps of faith, and why it can engage the fine-tuning and information arguments as legitimate natural theology. Dei Filius rules out both the fundamentalist who says science doesn't matter (fideism) and the atheist who says faith doesn't matter (rationalism).

Promulgated by: Pius IX · Vote: 533 in favor, 2 against, 55 "non placet" (those opposed largely absented themselves before the vote) · Structure: Preamble + 4 Chapters + Canons · Note: "First" Dogmatic Constitution because Vatican I intended to produce more on the Church — the Council was suspended when Italian forces seized Rome on September 20, 1870, and never reconvened. The "second" constitution came 95 years later as Lumen Gentium.

Historical Context: The most contested document in the Council's history. The immediate pressures: (1) The liberal democratic revolutions of 1848 had destabilized the Papal States and questioned all traditional authority; (2) Gallicanism — the French theological tradition asserting the independence of the French Church from Rome — was still alive; (3) Conciliarism's residue (a council has authority over the Pope) needed to be definitively settled; (4) The emerging democratic principle that all authority derives from the people challenged the hierarchical structure of the Church. 61 bishops formally opposed the definition of infallibility not because they denied it theologically but because they thought its definition was inopportune — too likely to cause rupture with the Eastern Churches and public hostility in liberal democracies. John Henry Newman, while privately convinced papal infallibility was true, publicly expressed regret that it was being defined, calling it "an aggressive insolent faction."

Chapter I — Institution of the Apostolic Primacy in Blessed Peter

1Christ did not leave the Church without a head but established Peter as the visible foundation and permanent principle of the Church's unity. He gave Peter alone the keys of the Kingdom (Matthew 16:18–19) and the commission to feed the whole flock (John 21:15–17) — a primacy not merely of honor or of moral influence but of genuine governance and jurisdiction over the universal Church.
2This primacy is not Peter's personal achievement or charismatic gift but an office — a function within the Church's structure that Christ himself instituted. It exists for the service of unity, not for the aggrandizement of the one who holds it.

Key Canon: "If anyone shall say that Blessed Peter the Apostle was not appointed by Christ the Lord as Prince of all the Apostles and visible Head of the whole Church Militant, or received only a primacy of honor and not one of true and proper jurisdiction — let him be anathema."

Chapter II — Perpetuity of the Primacy of Blessed Peter in the Roman Pontiffs

1The primacy established in Peter does not expire with Peter but descends perpetually to his successors as Bishop of Rome — the Roman Pontiffs. This is not an optional or contingent development but a matter of divine law: the need for a permanent, visible head of the Church's unity was not limited to the apostolic generation but is permanent.
2Whoever succeeds Peter in the See of Rome succeeds him in the primacy — not by election alone (the election identifies who succeeds) but by divine institution (the succession itself is divinely mandated). The primacy does not belong to the Roman Church by human convention but by Christ's own appointment.

Chapter III — The Power and Nature of the Primacy of the Roman Pontiff
